    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">\frac{1}{M}\nabla_{1}\cdot\nabla_{2}\,\!</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math>

is known as the ''mass polarization term'', which arises due to the motion of [[Atomic nucleus|atomic nuclei]]. The wavefunction is a function of the two electron's positions:

:<math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math" alttext="\psi=\psi(\mathbb{r}_{1},\mathbb{r}_{2})." xml:id="p1.1.m1.1" display="inline" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
  <semantics xml:id="p1.1.m1.1a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
    <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.cmml">
      <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.cmml">
        <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">ψ</mi>
        <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.2.cmml">=</mo>
        <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.cmml">
          <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.3" xref="p1.1.m1.1.3.cmml">ψ</mi>
          <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.1.cmml">⁢</mo>
          <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.cmml">
            <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.cmml">(</mo>
            <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2b" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.cmml">
              <msub xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.2.cmml">
                <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.5" xref="p1.1.m1.1.5.cmml">𝕣</mi>
                <mn xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.6.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.6.1.cmml">1</mn>
              </msub>
              <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2c" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.cmml">,</mo>
              <msub xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.3" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.3.cmml">
                <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.8" xref="p1.1.m1.1.8.cmml">𝕣</mi>
                <mn xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.9.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.9.1.cmml">2</mn>
              </msub>
            </mrow>
            <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2d" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.cmml">)</mo>
          </mrow>
        </mrow>
      </mrow>
      <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12b" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.cmml">.</mo>
    </mrow>
    <annotation-xml xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.cmml" encoding="MathML-Content" xref="p1.1.m1.1">
      <apply xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12">
        <eq xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.2.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.2"/>
        <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1">ψ</ci>
        <apply xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.1">
          <times xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.1"/>
          <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.3.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.3">ψ</ci>
          <apply xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2">
            <interval closure="open" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.1.cmml"/>
            <apply xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.2.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.2">
              <csymbol cd="ambiguous" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.2.1.cmml">subscript</csymbol>
              <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.5.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.5">𝕣</ci>
              <cn type="integer" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.6.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.6.1">1</cn>
            </apply>
            <apply xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.3.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.3">
              <csymbol cd="ambiguous" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.12.1.2.3.1.cmml">subscript</csymbol>
              <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.8.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.8">𝕣</ci>
              <cn type="integer" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.9.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.9.1">2</cn>
            </apply>
          </apply>
        </apply>
      </apply>
    </annotation-xml>
    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">\psi=\psi(\mathbb{r}_{1},\mathbb{r}_{2}).</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math>

There is no closed form solution for this equation.

===Time dependent===
This is the equation of motion for the quantum state. In the most general form, it is written:<ref name="Shankar 1994 143ff">{{cite book
 |last=Shankar |first=R.
 |year=1994
 |title=Principles of Quantum Mechanics
 |pages=143''ff''
 |publisher=[[Kluwer Academic]]/[[Plenum Publishers]]
 |isbn=978-0-306-44790-7
}}</ref>

    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">\begin{aligned}\displaystyle\hat{H}&amp;\displaystyle amp;=\sum_{{n=1}}^{{N}}\frac%
{\hat{p}_{n}^{2}}{2m_{n}}+V(x_{1},x_{2},\cdots x_{N},t)\\
&amp;\displaystyle amp;=-\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2}\sum_{{n=1}}^{{N}}\frac{1}{m_{n}}\frac%
{\partial^{2}}{\partial x_{n}^{2}}+V(x_{1},x_{2},\cdots x_{N},t)\end{aligned}</annotation>
  </semantics>

where the position of particle ''n'' is ''x<sub>n</sub>''.

                        <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.13.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.13.1">n</ci>
                      </apply>
                      <cn type="integer" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.14.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.14.1">2</cn>
                    </apply>
                    <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.15.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.15">normal-Ψ</ci>
                  </apply>
                </apply>
              </apply>
            </apply>
          </apply>
          <apply xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.19.2.2.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.19.2.2">
            <times xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.19.2.2.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.19.2.2.1"/>
            <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.17.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.17">V</ci>
            <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.18.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.18">normal-Ψ</ci>
          </apply>
        </apply>
      </apply>
    </annotation-xml>
    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">i\hbar\frac{\partial}{\partial t}\Psi=-\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2}\sum_{{n=1}}^{{N}}%
\frac{1}{m_{n}}\nabla_{n}^{2}\Psi+V\Psi</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math>

This last equation is in a very high dimension,<ref>

 |isbn=978-0-306-44790-7
}}</ref> so the solutions are not easy to visualize.

== Solution methods ==

{{multicol begin}}
General techniques:
* [[Perturbation theory (quantum mechanics)|Perturbation theory]]
* The [[variational method]]
* [[Quantum Monte Carlo]] methods
* [[Density functional theory]]
* The [[WKB approximation]] and semi-classical expansion
{{multicol-break}}

Methods for special cases:
* [[List of quantum-mechanical systems with analytical solutions]]
* [[Hartree–Fock]] method and [[post Hartree–Fock]] methods
{{multicol-end}}

== Properties ==

The Schrödinger equation has the following properties: some are useful, but there are shortcomings. Ultimately, these properties arise from the Hamiltonian used, and solutions to the equation.

===Linearity===

{{See also|Linear differential equation}}

In the development above, the Schrödinger equation was made to be linear for generality, though this has other implications. If two wave functions ''ψ''<sub>1</sub> and ''ψ''<sub>2</sub> are solutions, then so is any [[linear combination]] of the two:

:<math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math" alttext="\displaystyle\psi=a\psi_{1}+b\psi_{2}" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1" display="inline" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
  <semantics xml:id="p1.1.m1.1a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
    <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10" xref="p1.1.m1.1.10.cmml">
      <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">ψ</mi>
      <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.2.cmml">=</mo>
      <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.cmml">
        <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.1.cmml">
          <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.3" xref="p1.1.m1.1.3.cmml">a</mi>
          <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.1.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.1.1.cmml">⁢</mo>
          <msub xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.1.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.1.2.cmml">
            <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.4" xref="p1.1.m1.1.4.cmml">ψ</mi>
            <mn xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.5.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.5.1.cmml">1</mn>
          </msub>
        </mrow>
        <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.6" xref="p1.1.m1.1.6.cmml">+</mo>
        <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.2.cmml">
          <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.7" xref="p1.1.m1.1.7.cmml">b</mi>
          <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.2.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.2.1.cmml">⁢</mo>
          <msub xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.2.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.2.2.cmml">
            <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.8" xref="p1.1.m1.1.8.cmml">ψ</mi>
            <mn xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.9.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.9.1.cmml">2</mn>
          </msub>
        </mrow>
      </mrow>
    </mrow>
    <annotation-xml xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.cmml" encoding="MathML-Content" xref="p1.1.m1.1">
      <apply xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.10">
        <eq xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.2.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.2"/>
        <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1">ψ</ci>
        <apply xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.10.1">

            <times xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.1.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.1.1"/>
            <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.3.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.3">a</ci>
            <apply xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.1.2.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.1.2">
              <csymbol cd="ambiguous" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.1.2.1.cmml">subscript</csymbol>

            </apply>
          </apply>
          <apply xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.2.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.2">
            <times xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.2.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.2.1"/>
            <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.7.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.7">b</ci>
            <apply xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.2.2.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.2.2">
              <csymbol cd="ambiguous" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.10.1.2.2.1.cmml">subscript</csymbol>
              <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.8.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.8">ψ</ci>
              <cn type="integer" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.9.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.9.1">2</cn>
            </apply>
          </apply>
        </apply>
      </apply>
    </annotation-xml>
    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">\displaystyle\psi=a\psi_{1}+b\psi_{2}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math>

where ''a'' and ''b'' are any complex numbers (the sum can be extended for any number of wavefunctions). This property allows [[Quantum superposition|superpositions of quantum states]] to be solutions of the Schrödinger equation. In particular a given solution can be multiplied by any complex number, this allows one to solve for a wave function without normalizing it first.

=== Real energy eigenstates ===
For the time-independent equation, an additional feature of linearity follows: if two wave functions ''ψ''<sub>1</sub> and ''ψ''<sub>2</sub> are solutions to the time-independent equation with the same energy ''E'', then so is any linear combination:

:<math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math" alttext="\hat{H}(a\psi_{1}+b\psi_{2})=a\hat{H}\psi_{1}+b\hat{H}\psi_{2}=E(a\psi_{1}+b%&#10;\psi_{2})." xml:id="p1.1.m1.1" display="inline" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
  <semantics xml:id="p1.1.m1.1a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
    <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.33" xref="p1.1.m1.1.33.cmml">
      <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.33a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.33.cmml">
        <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.33.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.33.2.cmml">
          <mover accent="true" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">
            <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml">H</mi>
            <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml">^</mo>
          </mover>
          <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.33.2.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.33.2.1.cmml">⁢</mo>
          <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.33.2.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.33.2.2.cmml">
            <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.33.2.2a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.33.2.2.cmml">(</mo>
            <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.33.2.2b" xref="p1.1.m1.1.33.2.2.cmml">
              <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.33.2.2.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.33.2.2.1.cmml">
                <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.3" xref="p1.1.m1.1.3.cmml">a</mi>
                <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.33.2.2.1.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.33.2.2.1.1.cmml">⁢</mo>
                <msub xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.33.2.2.1.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.33.2.2.1.2.cmml">
                  <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.4" xref="p1.1.m1.1.4.cmml">ψ</mi>
                  <mn xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.5.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.5.1.cmml">1</mn>
                </msub>
              </mrow>

    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">\hat{H}(a\psi_{1}+b\psi_{2})=a\hat{H}\psi_{1}+b\hat{H}\psi_{2}=E(a\psi_{1}+b%
\psi_{2}).</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math>

Two different solutions with the same energy are called ''degenerate''.<ref name="Quantum Mechanics Demystified 2006"/> 

In an arbitrary potential, there is one degeneracy: if a wave function ''ψ'' solves the time-independent equation, so does its complex conjugate ''ψ''*. By taking linear combinations, the real and imaginary parts of ''ψ'' are each solutions. Thus, the time-independent eigenvalue problem can be restricted to real-valued wave functions.

In the time-dependent equation, complex conjugate waves move in opposite directions. If ''Ψ''(''x, t'') is one solution, then so is ''Ψ''(''x, –t''). The symmetry of complex conjugation is called [[T-symmetry|time-reversal symmetry]].

===Space and time derivatives===
[[File:Wavefunction continuity space.svg|250px|"250px"|thumb|Continuity of the wavefunction and its first spatial derivative (in the ''x'' direction, ''y'' and ''z'' coordinates not shown), at some time ''t''.]]

The Schrödinger equation is first order in time and second in space, which describes the time evolution of a quantum state (meaning it determines the future amplitude from the present). 

Explicitly for one particle in 3d Cartesian coordinates - the equation is

:<math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math" alttext="i\hbar{\partial\Psi\over\partial t}=-{\hbar^{2}\over 2m}\left({\partial^{2}%&#10;\Psi\over\partial x^{2}}+{\partial^{2}\Psi\over\partial y^{2}}+{\partial^{2}%&#10;\Psi\over\partial z^{2}}\right)+V(x,y,z,t)\Psi.\,\!" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1" display="inline" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
  <semantics xml:id="p1.1.m1.1a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
    <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.29" xref="p1.1.m1.1.29.cmml">

            <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.3.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.3.2.cmml">

    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">i\hbar{\partial\Psi\over\partial t}=-{\hbar^{2}\over 2m}\left({\partial^{2}%
\Psi\over\partial x^{2}}+{\partial^{2}\Psi\over\partial y^{2}}+{\partial^{2}%
\Psi\over\partial z^{2}}\right)+V(x,y,z,t)\Psi.\,\!</annotation>

The first time partial derivative implies the initial value (at ''t'' = 0) of the wavefunction 

:<math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math" alttext="\Psi(x,y,z,0)\,\!" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1" display="inline" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
  <semantics xml:id="p1.1.m1.1a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
    <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.13" xref="p1.1.m1.1.13.cmml">

      <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.13.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.13.2.cmml">
        <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.13.2a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.13.2.cmml">(</mo>
        <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.13.2b" xref="p1.1.m1.1.13.2.cmml">
          <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.3" xref="p1.1.m1.1.3.cmml">x</mi>

is an arbitrary constant. Likewise - the second order derivatives with respect to space implies the wavefunction ''and'' its first order spatial derivatives

    <annotation-xml xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.cmml" encoding="MathML-Content" xref="p1.1.m1.1">
      <mtext xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1">⁢amp⁢Ψxbybzbt⁢amp⁢∂∂xΨxbybzbt⁢∂∂yΨxbybzbt⁢∂∂zΨxbybzbt</mtext>
    </annotation-xml>
    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">\begin{aligned}&amp;\displaystyle amp;\Psi(x_{b},y_{b},z_{b},t)\\
&amp;\displaystyle amp;\frac{\partial}{\partial x}\Psi(x_{b},y_{b},z_{b},t)\quad%
\frac{\partial}{\partial y}\Psi(x_{b},y_{b},z_{b},t)\quad\frac{\partial}{%
\partial z}\Psi(x_{b},y_{b},z_{b},t)\end{aligned}\,\!</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math>

are all arbitrary constants at a given set of points, where ''x<sub>b</sub>, y<sub>b</sub>, z<sub>b</sub>'' are a set of points describing boundary ''b'' (derivatives are evaluated at the boundaries). Typically there are one or two boundaries, such as the [[step potential]] and [[particle in a box]] respectively. 

As the first order derivatives are arbitrary, the wavefunction can be a [[Smooth function|continuously differentiable function]] of space, since at any boundary the gradient of the wavefunction can be matched. The prominent case includes waves. 

On the contrary, wave equations in physics are usually ''second order in time'', notable are the family of classical [[wave equation]] and the quantum [[Klein–Gordon equation]].

=== Local conservation of probability ===

{{Main|Probability current|Continuity equation}}

The Schrödinger equation is consistent with [[conservation of probability|probability conservation]] - because it can directly derive the [[Continuity equation#Quantum mechanics|continuity equation]] for probability:<ref name="Quantum Mechanics 2004">Quantum Mechanics, E. Abers, Pearson Ed., Addison Wesley, Prentice Hall Inc, 2004, ISBN 978-0-13-146100-0</ref>
:<math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math" alttext="{\partial\over\partial t}\rho\left(\mathbb{r},t\right)+\nabla\cdot\mathbf{j}=0," xml:id="p1.1.m1.1" display="inline" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">

\Psi^{*}\right)\,\!</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math>
is the [[probability current]] (flow per unit area). 

Hence predictions from the Schrödinger equation do not violate probability conservation. However, the continuity equation is more fundamental and intuitive than the SE itself, and is always true, while SE is not.

=== Positive energy ===

If the potential is bounded from below, meaning there is a minimum value of potential energy, the eigenfunctions of the Schrödinger equation have energy which is also bounded from below. This can be seen most easily by using the [[variational principle]], as follows. (See also below).

For any linear operator <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math" alttext="\hat{A}" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1" display="inline" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
  <semantics xml:id="p1.1.m1.1a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
    <mover accent="true" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">
      <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml">A</mi>
      <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml">^</mo>
    </mover>
    <annotation-xml xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.cmml" encoding="MathML-Content" xref="p1.1.m1.1">
      <apply xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1">
        <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.1">normal-^</ci>
        <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.2">A</ci>
      </apply>
    </annotation-xml>
    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">\hat{A}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math> [[Bounded operator|bounded]] from below, the eigenvector with the smallest eigenvalue is the vector ''ψ'' that minimizes the quantity

:<math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math" alttext="\langle\psi|\hat{A}|\psi\rangle" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1" display="inline" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
  <semantics xml:id="p1.1.m1.1a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
    <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.8" xref="p1.1.m1.1.8.cmml">

        <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.2.cmml">ψ</mi>

      </mrow>
      <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.8.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.8.1.cmml">⁡</mo>

        <mover accent="true" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.4" xref="p1.1.m1.1.4.cmml">
          <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.4.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.4.2.cmml">A</mi>
          <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.4.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.4.1.cmml">^</mo>
        </mover>
        <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.8.3a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.8.3.cmml">⁡</mo>
        <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.8.3.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.8.3.1.cmml">
          <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.8.3.1a" fence="true" xref="p1.1.m1.1.8.3.1.cmml">|</mo>
          <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.6" xref="p1.1.m1.1.6.cmml">ψ</mi>
          <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.8.3.1b" xref="p1.1.m1.1.8.3.1.cmml">&gt;</mo>
        </mrow>
      </mrow>
    </mrow>
    <annotation-xml xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.cmml" encoding="MathML-Content" xref="p1.1.m1.1">
      <apply xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.8.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.8">

    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">\langle\psi|\hat{A}|\psi\rangle</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math>

over all ''ψ'' which are [[normalizable wave function|normalized]].<ref name="Quantum Mechanics 2004"/> In this way, the smallest eigenvalue is expressed through the [[variational principle]]. For the Schrödinger Hamiltonian <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math" alttext="\hat{H}" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1" display="inline" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
  <semantics xml:id="p1.1.m1.1a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
    <mover accent="true" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">
      <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml">H</mi>
      <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml">^</mo>
    </mover>
    <annotation-xml xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.cmml" encoding="MathML-Content" xref="p1.1.m1.1">
      <apply xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1">
        <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.1">normal-^</ci>
        <ci xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.2">H</ci>
      </apply>
    </annotation-xml>
    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">\hat{H}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math> bounded from below, the smallest eigenvalue is called the ground state energy. That energy is the minimum value of

    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">\langle\psi|\hat{H}|\psi\rangle=\int\psi^{*}(\mathbb{r})\left[-\frac{\hbar^{2}%
}{2m}\nabla^{2}\psi(\mathbb{r})+V(\mathbb{r})\psi(\mathbb{r})\right]d^{3}%
\mathbb{r}=\int\left[\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2m}|\nabla\psi|^{2}+V(\mathbb{r})|\psi|^%
{2}\right]d^{3}\mathbb{r}=\langle\hat{H}\rangle</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math>

(using [[integration by parts]]). Due to the [[Absolute value#Complex numbers|complex modulus]] of ''ψ'' squared (which is positive definite), the right hand side always greater than the lowest value of ''V''(''x''). In particular, the ground state energy is positive when ''V''(''x'') is everywhere positive. 

For potentials which are bounded below and are not infinite over a region, there is a ground state which minimizes the integral above. This lowest energy wavefunction is real and positive definite - meaning the wavefunction can increase and decrease, but is positive for all positions. It physically cannot be negative: if it were, smoothing out the bends at the sign change (to minimize the wavefunction) rapidly reduces the gradient contribution to the integral and hence the kinetic energy, while the potential energy changes linearly and less quickly. The kinetic and potential energy are both changing at different rates, so the total energy is not constant, which can't happen (conservation). The solutions are consistent with Schrödinger equation if this wavefunction is positive definite.

The lack of sign changes also shows that the ground state is nondegenerate, since if there were two ground states with common energy ''E'', not proportional to each other, there would be a linear combination of the two that would also be a ground state resulting in a zero solution.

===Analytic continuation to diffusion===

The above properties (positive definiteness of energy) allow the [[analytic continuation]] of the Schrödinger equation to be identified as a [[stochastic process]], which can be represented by a [[path integral formulation#The Schrödinger equation|path integral]]. This can be interpreted as the [[Huygens–Fresnel principle]] applied to De Broglie waves, the spreading wavefronts are diffusive probability amplitudes.<ref name="Quantum Mechanics 2004"/> 

For a particle which is [[random walk]]ing (again for which ''V'' = 0), the continuation is to let:<ref>http://www.stt.msu.edu/~mcubed/Relativistic.pdf</ref>

:<math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math" alttext="\tau=it\,\!" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1" display="inline" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">

which has the same form as the [[Diffusion equation]], with diffusion coefficient ''ħ''/2''m''.

===Galilean and Lorentz transformations===
====Non-relativistic====
{{main|Galilean invariance}}

The solutions to the Schrödinger equation are not [[Galilean invariance|Galilean invariant]], so the equation itself is not either, as outlined below. Changing inertial reference frames requires a transformation of the wavefunction analogous to requiring [[gauge invariance]]. This transformation introduces a phase factor that is normally ignored as non-physical, but has application in some problems.<ref>{{cite journal|first= A. B. |last=van Oosten |title=Covariance of the Schrödinger equation under low velocity boost |journal=Apeiro |pages=449&ndash;454 |volume=13 |number=2 |year=2006}}</ref>

[[Galilean transformations]] (or "boosts") look at the system from the point of view of an observer moving with a steady velocity –''v''. A boost must change the physical properties of a wavepacket in the same way as in classical mechanics:<ref name="Quantum Mechanics 2004"/>

:<math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math" alttext="\mathbb{r}^{{\prime}}=\mathbb{r}+\mathbb{v}t\quad\mathbb{p}^{{\prime}}=\mathbb%&#10;{p}+m\mathbb{v}.\," xml:id="p1.1.m1.1" display="inline" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
  <semantics xml:id="p1.1.m1.1a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">

          </apply>
        </apply>
      </apply>
    </annotation-xml>
    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">\mathbb{r}^{{\prime}}=\mathbb{r}+\mathbb{v}t\quad\mathbb{p}^{{\prime}}=\mathbb%
{p}+m\mathbb{v}.\,</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math>

For a free-particle solution (plane wave), Galilean invariance requires the [[phase (waves)|phase]] ([[Argument (complex analysis)|complex exponent]]) to remain in the same form:

but instead the phase terms transform according to

:<math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math" alttext="\begin{aligned}\displaystyle(\mathbb{p}\cdot\mathbb{r}-Et)/\hbar&amp;\displaystyle&#10;amp%&#10;;\rightarrow\left[(\mathbb{p}^{{\prime}}-m\mathbb{v})\cdot(\mathbb{r}^{{\prime%&#10;}}-\mathbb{v}t)-(\mathbb{p}^{{\prime}}-m\mathbb{v})\cdot(\mathbb{p}^{{\prime}}%&#10;-m\mathbb{v})t/2m\right]/\hbar\\&#10;&amp;\displaystyle amp;=\left[(\mathbb{p}^{{\prime}}\cdot\mathbb{r}^{{\prime}}+E^{%&#10;{\prime}}t)+(\mathbb{p}\cdot\mathbb{r}-Et)\right]/\hbar\\&#10;\end{aligned}" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1" display="inline" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
  <semantics xml:id="p1.1.m1.1a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
    <mtable rowspacing="0.2ex" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1" columnspacing="0.4em" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">
      <mtr xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">
        <mtd columnalign="right" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1b" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">
          <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1">
            <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1.11">
              <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1.11a">(</mo>
              <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1.11b">
                <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1.11.1">
                  <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1.2">𝕡</mi>
                  <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1.3">⋅</mo>
                  <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1.4">𝕣</mi>
                </mrow>
                <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1.5">-</mo>
                <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1.11.2">
                  <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1.6">E</mi>
                  <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1.11.2.1">⁢</mo>
                  <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1.7">t</mi>
                </mrow>
              </mrow>
              <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1.11c">)</mo>
            </mrow>
            <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1.9">/</mo>
            <mi mathvariant="normal" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.1.10">ℏ</mi>
          </mrow>
        </mtd>
        <mtd columnalign="left" xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1c" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">
          <mrow xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.2.1e">
            <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.2.1.1">a</mi>
            <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.2.1.2">m</mi>
            <mi xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.2.1.3">p</mi>
            <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.2.1.4">;</mo>
            <mo xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.1.2.1.5">→</mo>

    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">\begin{aligned}\displaystyle(\mathbb{p}\cdot\mathbb{r}-Et)/\hbar&amp;\displaystyle
amp%
;\rightarrow\left[(\mathbb{p}^{{\prime}}-m\mathbb{v})\cdot(\mathbb{r}^{{\prime%
}}-\mathbb{v}t)-(\mathbb{p}^{{\prime}}-m\mathbb{v})\cdot(\mathbb{p}^{{\prime}}%
-m\mathbb{v})t/2m\right]/\hbar\\
&amp;\displaystyle amp;=\left[(\mathbb{p}^{{\prime}}\cdot\mathbb{r}^{{\prime}}+E^{%
{\prime}}t)+(\mathbb{p}\cdot\mathbb{r}-Et)\right]/\hbar\\
\end{aligned}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math>

(energy is kinetic energy, since ''V'' = 0) therefore the plane wave transforms by

      <mtext xml:id="p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="p1.1.m1.1.1">=Ψ⁢Ae/⁢i-⋅pr⁢Etℏamp;→⁢ampΨ′amp;=Ae/⁢i-⋅p′r′⁢E′tℏe/⁢i-⋅pr⁢Etℏ⁢amp⁢ampamp;=Ψe/⁢i-⋅p′r′⁢E′tℏ⁢amp⁢ampamp;≠Ae/⁢i-⋅p′r′⁢E′tℏ.</mtext>
    </annotation-xml>
    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">\begin{aligned}\displaystyle\par
\Psi=Ae^{{i(\mathbb{p}\cdot\mathbb{r}-Et)/%
\hbar}}&amp;\displaystyle amp;\rightarrow&amp;\displaystyle amp;\Psi^{{\prime}}&amp;%
\displaystyle amp;=Ae^{{i(\mathbb{p}^{{\prime}}\cdot\mathbb{r}^{{\prime}}-E^{{%
\prime}}t)/\hbar}}e^{{i(\mathbb{p}\cdot\mathbb{r}-Et)/\hbar}}\\
&amp;\displaystyle amp;&amp;\displaystyle amp;&amp;\displaystyle amp;=\Psi e^{{i(\mathbb{p%
}^{{\prime}}\cdot\mathbb{r}^{{\prime}}-E^{{\prime}}t)/\hbar}}\\
&amp;\displaystyle amp;&amp;\displaystyle amp;&amp;\displaystyle amp;\neq Ae^{{i(\mathbb{p%
}^{{\prime}}\cdot\mathbb{r}^{{\prime}}-E^{{\prime}}t)/\hbar}}.\\
\displaystyle\par
\end{aligned}\,\!</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math>

So by [[proof by contradiction|contradiction]], the extra phase factor implies the Schrödinger equation is not Galilean invariant: the plane wave solutions do not remain in the same form under Galilean transformations. A linear combination of plane waves, with different values of '''p''' and ''E'', will also transform in the same way due to linearity. In general the transformation of any solution to the free-particle Schrödinger equation, ''Ψ''('''r''', ''t'') results in other "boosted" solutions:

:<math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math" alttext="\Psi^{{\prime}}(\mathbb{r}^{{\prime}},t)=\Psi(\mathbb{r},t)e^{{i(\mathbb{p}^{{%&#10;\prime}}\cdot\mathbb{r}^{{\prime}}-E^{{\prime}}t)/\hbar}}=\Psi(\mathbb{r}^{{%&#10;\prime}}-\mathbb{v}t,t)e^{{i(\mathbb{p}^{{\prime}}\cdot\mathbb{r}^{{\prime}}-E%&#10;^{{\prime}}t)/\hbar}}.\," xml:id="p1.1.m1.1" display="inline" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">

====Relativistic====

{{main|Lorentz invariance}}

The [[Lorentz transformations]] are (slightly) more complicated than the Galilean ones, so the solutions to the Schrödinger equation are certainly not [[Lorentz invariance|Lorentz invariant]] either, in turn not consistent with [[special relativity]]. Also, as shown above in the plausibility argument - the Schrödinger equation was constructed from ''classical'' energy conservation rather than the ''relativistic'' [[invariant mass|mass–energy relation]]

:<math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math" alttext="E^{2}=(pc)^{2}+(m_{0}c^{2})^{2}\,." xml:id="p1.1.m1.1" display="inline" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">
  <semantics xml:id="p1.1.m1.1a" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">

    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">E^{2}=(pc)^{2}+(m_{0}c^{2})^{2}\,.</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math>

This relativistic equation ''is'' Lorentz invariant. The classical equation is not - it is the low-velocity limit of the relativistic equation (velocities much less than the [[speed of light]]). This further shows that the Schrödinger equation itself, not just the solutions, is not Lorentz invariant.

Secondly, the equation requires the particles to be the same type, and the number of particles in the system to be constant, since their masses are constants in the equation (kinetic energy terms).<ref name="autogenerated1">Quantum Field Theory, D. McMahon, Mc Graw Hill (USA), 2008, ISBN 978-0-07-154382-8</ref> This alone means the Schrödinger equation is not compatible with relativity - even the simple equation

    <annotation xml:id="p1.1.m1.1b" encoding="application/x-tex" xref="p1.1.m1.1.cmml">\displaystyle E=mc^{2}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math>

allows (in [[High energy physics|high-energy processes]]) particles of matter to completely transform into energy by particle-antiparticle [[annihilation]], and enough energy can re-create other particle-antiparticle pairs. So the number of particles and types of particles is not necessarily fixed. For all other intrinsic properties of the particles which may enter the potential function, including mass (such as the harmonic oscillator) and charge (such as electrons in atoms), which will also be constants in the equation, the same problem follows.

====Extension and generalization====

In order to extend Schrödinger's formalism to include relativity, the physical picture must be transformed. The [[Klein–Gordon equation]] and the [[Dirac equation]] ''are'' built from the relativistic mass–energy relation; so as a result these equations are relativistically invariant, and replace the Schrödinger equation in [[relativistic quantum mechanics]]. In attempt to extend the scope of these equations further, other [[relativistic wave equations]] have developed. By no means is the Schrödinger equation obsolete: it is still in use for both teaching and research - particularly in physical and quantum chemistry to understand the properties of atoms and molecules, but understood to be an approximation to real behaviour of them, for speeds much less than [[speed of light|light]].
